I put all the ingredients in a bowl, mixed it with the stick end of a wooden spoon, kneaded it in the bowl just to make sure everything was blended, put a cover over it and left it overnight, put it in a bread pan after kneading in the bowl for a couple of minues, let it rise until it was a bit over the top of the pan, baked it for 35 minutes, perfection ! (I used the "Steve" method of 2 bread pans kept together with large bull clips to get the dutch oven effect for bread making).

Jenny: if you are reading this I made your "no kneed" bread recipie using wheat flour instead of white flour. Please do yourself a favor and buy 1b SAS (or other brand INSTANT) Yeast and store in freezer (in mason jar) till you need it.  You will likely have enough for 2 years if you keep it in freezer till you spoon it out into recipe.  Much cheaper and I've never had a fail with SAS brand as long as I didn't add too hot of a liquid.  INSTANT does not mean RAPID RISE.  It simply indicates it instantly hydrates and can be added directly to dry ingredients.  Does not need to be activated in water like some yeasts.   Those store bought packages are expensive AND while this type of yeast can get you a bread in 90 mins....WHY do you need it that fast?    Slower (say bread with 1 rest and 1 rise, at maybe double the timing,  means you will have less stomach upset because the nature will work on the gluten while the yeast works.  Slower fermentation means a better storage loaf.  Not so important if you have no kids under 2 years eating it, or are only making 1 loaf for 1-2 days only.   Old baker here.

I've made this bread a few times and while it does come together as easily as shown, it's not very good.  It looks nice and the crust is great, but the crumb leaves something to be desired.  Thirty-five minutes for the rise just isn't enough time for the gluten structure to develop, and so you're left with a very crumbly bread.  It's doesn't have that nice chewy texture you expect inside a slice of bread.  Next time I make this, I'm going to autolyse the dough for an hour and then mix the yeast in and do a series of stretch/fold/rises like you would for sourdough to see if I can get a better gluten structure to develop

Hi!  This is my first Post and I'm hoping I can find some help here.  I have been making no-knead breads using bread flower have also used bread flower with 25% whole wheat & 10% Rye. I have used quick acting, instant, & sourdough on different loaves. I have used the 1/4 to 1/2 tsp yeast with cool water & 8-24hr, 72deg 1st proofing as well as the 1 1/4 to 2 tsp yeast with warm water & 1 to 3 hr, 85deg 1st proofing. Everything through this step seems OK, My dough has lots of bubbles and has more than doubled in volume  I degas, fold, & stretch the dough and place in a parchment lined container of appropriate size and shape for my 5qt dutch oven or my superstone bread baker. I have a bread machine, a rather less known brand, "Strata".   I paid about $35. for it including shipping.  Its recipe book includes a 50% whole wheat bread.   Jenny's is about 73% whole wheat by volume.  I prefer the whole wheat texture, but I also like a bread that rises well for a lighter weight texture.  Also,  I entered uncharted territory, using a machine rather than kneading by hand.   My machine's program takes 4:05 hours.   The time was not an important factor for me.  I tried it, adding the liquid ingredients, warm milk, whole egg, oil, salt, and sugar first, then the flours and the yeast.  As the dough mixed,  I added a level tablespoon of bread flour.   About four hours later,  I had a fine loaf of 73% whole wheat bread.   It had risen and baked fully, yielding 13, 1/2" slices of light-weight, brown crusted, tasty bread, plus a thinner crown slice.   Thanks, Jenny!   P.S.  My "Strata" produces a vertical loaf, all square slices of the same size, which I prefer.    Instead of "spanking" the dough,  I did some shopping and my laundry.   You can spank me, Jenny!

And yes, what a coincidence because I decided to fix my bread that happens to be the same technique using only one rise in the bread pan.  But my bread did get a period of rest because I had to do something else really quick before I put it in the pan, maybe 5 minutes.  Since I'm in a cooler environment I had to let my bread rise longer..  But, my bread was made almost exactly like your bread except mine was a barley white bread.  And like yours it continued to rise after baking.  But after I baked it, I'm not so sure if I like my bread that fluffy so I may go back to the 2 rise method.     I've learned how to read my dough and make adjustments if there are adjustments need to be made so I don't have to put flour on my board.  Yeah, the dough will stick to your fingers which you have to scrap off but eventually it stops sticking to your hands and boards when kneaded long enough. I'm not fortunate enough to have a nice Kitchen Aid Stand Mixer so I knead for 10 to 12 minutes.  Actually what I would love to have is a KMix stand mixer with the Kn Tool. Allowing the dough to proof a longer time will take the kneading out of it.  Using warm milk speeds up the process.  About four hours proofing will have it almost ready.  Put it in a skillet to shape and proof for another hour, slide it into the loaf pan and into the stove with zero kneading.   Remember that yeast is alive, boiling or near boiling water or milk will kill it. 120 f is perfect.  If you need the bread in an hour and a half, kneading will be involved. The fun part for some folks.  We don't buy bread anymore either and it is a significant savings, especially for the fancy stuff like olive bread.

Ok, the egg thing was hilarious. As a baking enthusiast though, I have to take issue with 2 points. To be accurate this is a Transitional Whole Wheat bread. They are more to popular liking than true whole grain breads. Second, bread tastes so much better when it has been allowed to rise properly. I know the time factor seems daunting to some people, but using extra yeast to speed it up affects the flavor. It can leave it tasting a bit metallic. With half the yeast and twice the time, this is an excellent recipe.

At 01:55, MORE flour that is NOT whole wheat. I'm not interested in this video now.
"...RapidRise instant yeast, but the regular active yeast is the same." NOT true. They are cultivated differently. RapidRise works faster. I know, because I've tried both many times. Yes, the bread that comes out of the oven is the same, but the time to wait for the rise is different... UNLESS some conditions (room temperature, etc.) was different. If the room was cooler the day you used the RapidRise, it will not rise as rapidly.
TECNICALLY when you add flour that is NOT whole wheat flour, you are making what the bakeries and supermarkets in the U.S. call "Wheat Bread", NOT "WHOLE wheat bread". It may be 90% whole wheat, but it isn't whole wheat. This is different from our calling Obama "African American", when he was only half.

This recipe is brilliant. I've made 5-6 loaves of bread (wheat and white based on the first two cups of flour) with it and other than the slight variation I will note below, it's worked perfectly every time. This bread is exactly as good as more complicated recipes I've used that require 2 or more hours of rising and lots more tiresome kneading.   I will say that before I found this recipe I spent around a year experimenting on and off, and I do think bread normally requires a little patience, finesse, and experience, but this is the quickest, easiest recipe with consistently good results, and you can almost whip up bread on a quick whim instead of having to decide to do it ahead of time. (Seriously, other recipes you need to start no later than the afternoon if you want the bread that night.)  I made most of my loaves last summer and the recipe worked exactly as she said, one 35 minute rise other than the ten minute rest before shaping the loaf.  I just made another wheat loaf tonight (this is in late December) and the only difference is the rise seemed to be going nowhere after 15 minutes. I believe this is a combo of the same jar of yeast being 4 or 5 months older, and the fact that it's under 30 degrees outside and the kitchen is colder. When I noticed the bread wasn't rising, I put it covered on top of the stove and preheated the oven under it to 375. The rise ended up taking a total of 45-50 minutes but it finally got there (if I'd have turned on the stove at the beginning it likely would have been the normal 35 min.) I believe Jenni is in CA without such outdoor temperature variation, so she might not have experienced this.   My tips - you don't want the milk "hot". If you don't have a thermometer, get it luke-warm. Think of the temperature you'd want to give milk to a baby; just barely warm but unable to cause pain if you touch it. Milk that's too hot will kill your yeast.   I have accidentally forgotten to add the egg to this recipe and it didn't really seem to hurt anything, so don't let lack of eggs stop you from trying this. It might be more important when you use whole wheat flour with less gluten - just make sure there's some regular flour in the mix (the last half cup, then the 1/4 cup you add at the end of the mix.) Normal bread recipes do not usually call for an egg, so it does seem to be just insurance to tighten the inner crumb.
You can warm an egg is by putting for a couple of minutes in hot/warm tap water. And I use the hook for the dough mixer,  and I let it knead it for 5 min. It shuts off by himself. No hand kneading. I am not good at counting, as I don't know what language to use, LOL

Great video, thanks. Technically, whole wheat (especially fresh ground) has less gluten than processed white bread flour. The reason is the whole grain is used which by weight results in less gluten. Consequently, many whole wheat recipes recommend the addition of a tablespoon or so of gluten.

Jenny, I love your videos, you seem to always make me giggle! Btw, it shouldn't matter if the shiny side or the dull side of the foil is covering. Aluminum is aluminum and it has the same heat transference between its atoms and the melting point of aluminum will always remain the same. The foil is only dull on one side and shiny on the other because of the process of making foil by the manufacturer. But you can experiment though to see if I am wrong!

Every professional baker use cold water, cause of the frictionheat you get from the machine. its a thumbrule that every minute adds 1 degree celcius and the perfect dough is 24 celcius. The cold water is because you want to mix it so the gluten gets strong and that is the secret of making a good and fluffy bread.  Not this egg/milk/sugar unhealthy bread you made. haha, love the egg routine. bread looks good too.
I needed to make some bread but I've been dreading it because of the heat, the long kneading times, and my general patience in cooking (or lack there of) since I work at home. I'm picky about my whole wheat bread, and much of the commercial kinds I can't eat because I'm highly allergic to honey. So in short, I wasn't expecting much.  And then I discovered that my husband failed to tell me that he had used the last of the all-purpose flour. Needless to say I wasn't happy. Being out of options I replaced the AP flour with whole wheat and whisked in 1/4 c. vital wheat gluten before proceeded with the recipe. Once I started mixing the dough, I realized that I should have waited to put the other 1/2 c. WW flour in as it quickly formed a ball. The rest of the process went on without any more unexpected deviations. I have to say I'm in love with the minimal kneading, something my wrists really appreciate.  Once the bread came out of the oven it looked PERFECT! I couldn't believe it was even soft on top after I was bracing myself for another thick crusted wheat bread (that ends up feeling like eating shrapnel.) The best part is I didn't feel trapped in the kitchen and I was able to have homemade bread done for my husband's lunch before he left for work.
